<div id="review_475851555" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/475851555" />
    <a title="Kate Sherrod" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1854297-kate-sherrod"><img alt="Kate Sherrod" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442598495p2/1854297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/475851555?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate Sherrod" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate Sherrod" href="/user/show/1854297-kate-sherrod">Kate Sherrod</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780345364630" href="/book/show/1368922.The_Quincunx">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer475851555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16119154768493949436">I suppose we could regard Charles Palliser's Quincunx as final proof that for every genre or great genre master of fiction, however obscure or archaic, there is not only someone who will attempt a pastiche of it/him, but sometimes there is even one who is very, very good at it. Charles Palliser is one of these, an otaku's otaku in the realm of... the nineteenth century social novel?<br/><br/>I didn't know there could be such a thing. Did you?<br/><br/>For Quincunx* is a Dickensian pastiche of the very highest order</span>
  <span id="freeText16119154768493949436" style="display:none">I suppose we could regard Charles Palliser's Quincunx as final proof that for every genre or great genre master of fiction, however obscure or archaic, there is not only someone who will attempt a pastiche of it/him, but sometimes there is even one who is very, very good at it. Charles Palliser is one of these, an otaku's otaku in the realm of... the nineteenth century social novel?<br /><br />I didn't know there could be such a thing. Did you?<br /><br />For Quincunx* is a Dickensian pastiche of the very highest order, though it goes Dickens one better, or at least earlier, by setting itself in Britain's late Regency (and therefore pre-Victorian by a good bit) period. And perhaps it takes the Dickens to 11 at the very least, both in terms of legal/inheritance wrangling as plot driver and of risible degrees and numbers of coincidences at least in that Dickens' and Palliser's Londons have hilariously small populations.<br /><br />And there is still more to keep the 21st century reader chuckling, for about halfway through, when a certain heraldry puzzle assumes paramount importance, the penny drops and one realizes she is in fact reading a high quality prose version of a hidden object game. All that is missing is the frustrating experience of "breaking" the cursor by mis-clicking on too many objects, but then again, that could be substituted for by our young hero's continually narrowly escaping yet another assassination attempt -- or only sort of escaping, continually forced as he is to more or less respawn as the penniless, near-helpless, delerious, paranoid, starving waif that he is for most of the novel.<br /><br />And why is this so? Because, as I said, property inheritance and greed are the great drivers of the plot. An ancient francophone family (lots of glorious surnames feature in this story: Umphraville, Palphramond, Mompesson!) whose possession of a profitable estate dates back, apparently, to the time of William the Conqueror and whose bloodline includes Plantagenet ancestry, fell on hard times a few generations before our hero (John Mellamphy, he who answers to oh so many other names as he grows up) was born has been shadow-fighting over different versions of the patriarch's will in addition to publicly battling it out in the Court of Chancery (shades of Jarndyce and Jarndyce, eh wot?). Depending on what document finally surfaces and is approved, one family could be turned off the estate in favor of, well, several others.<br /><br />The plot is intricate and small details matter; like in playing a hidden object game, we have to scrutinize every scene with care, somewhat hampered by our guide through all of this, John of the Many Surnames, from whom Secrets Have Been Kept and whose life is perpetually both endangered and protected by different interests, depending on which will from which they would benefit.<br /><br />All this and all the Dickensian social justice hand-wringing you could ask for, as we spend time with body snatchers, dishonest bankers and lawyers, out-and-out bandit gangs, "down below men" who make their living salvaging coins and other valuables that have fallen into the sewers, starving Victorian garment workers, and, every once in a while, the gentry living high and betting too much on cards and horses. Like you do.<br /><br />If that sounds like something you might enjoy, you'll enjoy the hell out of this book. I did, even though I snickered a lot. Hey, snickering is good.<br /><br />*And I absolutely wound up reading this one now because of Aliette de Bodard, whose Obsidian and Blood Aztec godpunk trilogy employs the visual device and term of "quincunx" - a five-fold cross, more or less - and every time I came across the term, I remembered that my mother had presented me with a battered but still nice hardcover edition of Quincunx and it was still the substantial base of my small but formidable tower of dead tree TBR. Brains are funny old things.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16119154768493949436" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2200839169" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16310273247362592695">I am done. Five stars. Long and windy, knotted up family histories, inheritances, betrayals, greed and power, estate law and wills, so much London, and mostly the nasty bits you don't often read about. It's not perfect, most of the characters make you want to throttle them in some way, but intricately researched and planned, spirals upon webs of inter-connected plots and people that was so satisfying to see come together. I read nearly 1000 pages in a month so it tells me something. Like Bleak H</span>
  <span id="freeText16310273247362592695" style="display:none">I am done. Five stars. Long and windy, knotted up family histories, inheritances, betrayals, greed and power, estate law and wills, so much London, and mostly the nasty bits you don't often read about. It's not perfect, most of the characters make you want to throttle them in some way, but intricately researched and planned, spirals upon webs of inter-connected plots and people that was so satisfying to see come together. I read nearly 1000 pages in a month so it tells me something. Like Bleak House and The Woman in White and a bunch of other things dashed in as well. I like Charles Palliser.<br /><br /> </span>

<div id="review_130485997" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/130485997" />
    <a title="M.M. Bennetts" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4534038-m-m-bennetts"><img alt="M.M. Bennetts" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442512823p2/4534038.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/130485997?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M.M. Bennetts" class="user" itemprop="url" name="M.M. Bennetts" href="/user/show/4534038-m-m-bennetts">M.M. Bennetts</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer130485997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6041961015238979526"><i>This review was originally published in <b>The Christian Science Monitor</b>.</i><br/><br/>Think, if you will, of an earlier age in the chronicles of English literature–an age when authors such as Dickens, Thackeray and Trollope flourished. Remember a period in which verbosity was no crime and a novel was needed to fill the long spare hours of autumn evenings. Recall that time when themes were veiled in multiple layers of plot and characters, that era in which an author might freely discourse with his reader.<br/><br/>It is t</span>
  <span id="freeText6041961015238979526" style="display:none"><i>This review was originally published in <b>The Christian Science Monitor</b>.</i><br /><br />Think, if you will, of an earlier age in the chronicles of English literature–an age when authors such as Dickens, Thackeray and Trollope flourished. Remember a period in which verbosity was no crime and a novel was needed to fill the long spare hours of autumn evenings. Recall that time when themes were veiled in multiple layers of plot and characters, that era in which an author might freely discourse with his reader.<br /><br />It is to this previous epoch, more than to our own, that <b>The Quincunx</b>, Charles Palliser’s novel of early 19th-century England belongs.<br /><br />The Oxford English Dictionary defines ‘quincunx’ as ‘an arrangement or disposition of five objects so placed that the four occupy the corners, and the fifth the centre’. In the context of Palliser’s book, the quincunx is an heraldic device of five fleurs representing the five branches of a single family–the heirs of one Henry Huffam. <br /><br />For a decade, the Huffam heirs have feuded, employing both legal and criminal means, for the control of a vast fortune, the disposition of which depends upon the suppression, destruction or revelation of a codicil to a will.<br /><br />The central figure of Palliser’s quincunx is John Mellamphy. He is an child at the novel’s outset, with neither father nor surname, but the rightful heir under the terms of the codicil. His mother–the timid and trusting woman who holds the coveted codicil–is bilked of her small income through the machinations of her father-in-law. Desiring safety from their enemies, and seeking some measure of economic support, Mrs. Mellamphy takes her son to London. Instead of finding succor, she places herself and her son in further danger.<br /><br />Swindled and humiliated, the Mellamphys are reduced to a life of meanest poverty, scrabbling a meagre living on the fringes of London’s depraved and criminal underworld. Mrs. Mellamphy finds release in drug addiction. Buffeted between despair and danger by foes bent on his destruction, the boy joins the ranks of the homeless, begging for bread, sleeping in doorways and gutted buildings. Doggedly, he pursues the truth of his paternity and the resolution to the issue of the codicil and the Huffam inheritance. But this is only the surface of the plot.<br /><br />The author has peopled this dark realm of avarice and equity with vivid characters. In the style of Dickens, Palliser melds the metaphoric and realistic, creating a rich concert of humanity caught in the grip of universal, unfaltering truths.<br /><br />Throughout this cathartic epic, Palliser’s tone and language remain locked in the Victorian aesthetic. His narrative voice is sharp and stark, slicing clear etchings of that world of glimmering half-light and distorted lime-lit shadows we glimpse in sepia daguerreotypes. It is a prose style more familiar to readers of Thackeray, lacking the sentimentality of Dickens.<br /><br />As in Dickens’ <b>Bleak House</b>, <b>The Quincunx</b> explores the vast cavern of corruption and greed within the context of property and equity under the law. Like him, Palliser has woven the components of a gripping mystery into a substantial work of social, historical and literary merit. <br /><br /><b>The Quincunx</b> disturbs, provokes, enthralls, enriches, entertains, and reveals truth. It is, indeed, a masterpiece.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6041961015238979526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13885460640038689592">This book was highly recommended to me by a person whose taste I usually share, but I can't get behind him on this one.   <span id="freeText13885460640038689592" style="display:none">This book was highly recommended to me by a person whose taste I usually share, but I can't get behind him on this one. After about 300 pages of meandering yet overstuffed plot, complex and uninteresting legal jargon, and some characters who made me literally want to throw the book across the room (the narrator's mother is a huge problem - as a character her only traits are her weakness, naivety, and incredible stupidity. What it looked like instead was that I was in for 800 pages of the same, although at least the mother dies at some point. Sorry, book, you've gotten enough of my time. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4783221557588114331">My first read from this author and I had high hopes for this book. I loved the length, the topic and the time period. I ended up sorely disappointed.<br/><br/>The author, obviously, is a fan of Dickens. The language and "feel" of the book definitely spoke that clearly.   <span id="freeText4783221557588114331" style="display:none">My first read from this author and I had high hopes for this book. I loved the length, the topic and the time period. I ended up sorely disappointed.<br /><br />The author, obviously, is a fan of Dickens. The language and "feel" of the book definitely spoke that clearly. However, after the 27th time that John got abducted/beat up/wronged/double-crossed, I gave up (and there were at least another 30 times it happened after that!). I get the down and despair. I get that it was a struggle for him to make his way through the madness. But really? <br /><br />The ending? Hugely disappointing. It really can't even be called an "end", because there is no end. Nothing is resolved. No decisions are made. The words just die off, and you are left with nothing. I'm not asking for a fairy tale ending or that everyone lives "happily ever after" (which anyone who reads this book would never buy), but give me a tiny bit of closure at least.<br /><br />It has been a long while since I have read a book and am left with the feeling that I wasted my time. I'm very careful about what I choose to read simply because I have limited time. This book left me feeling just that though. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4783221557588114331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer8026290221358232582">WOW, talk about mystery! This book was full of twists and turns. I couldn't put it down. It was definitely a little confusing determining the family lineage at times, and I got confused with how inter-connected every character was in the novel. But it was a joy to read. It follows the life of a young London boy, and all the travesties, mostly, for there was little joy in his life, that he miraculously overcame either by his own wits or the help of people he knew little of.   <span id="freeText8026290221358232582" style="display:none">WOW, talk about mystery! This book was full of twists and turns. I couldn't put it down. It was definitely a little confusing determining the family lineage at times, and I got confused with how inter-connected every character was in the novel. But it was a joy to read. It follows the life of a young London boy, and all the travesties, mostly, for there was little joy in his life, that he miraculously overcame either by his own wits or the help of people he knew little of. It starts with his happy childhood, which quickly turns to the worse once he realizes that he and his mother are in danger because of their being named benefactors in a will. They travel to London to escape their pursuers, but are endlessly faced with problems once they reach that destination. It was a very detailed account of every scene, and I thoroughly enjoyed it. If you love historical mystery fiction, this is definitely the book for you! </span>
  <a data-text-id="8026290221358232582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vMW+La056hGc2XNXt+dn5159wzA487sd7SADth0bJxsTYt7DgJm5PD96wiV6PzCJ1J4X/vPfNp87KOe2WjQVQA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('M3pCLfgKhYZf25aoWuRpE2dBzvuuc+ajktaFo48liquc3SLD1arWq/x4J9qXPD597aIaNWVfayFE3mGjyAq48A==')})
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_3562159'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3562159-the-wrong-house?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Wrong House: The Architecture of Alfred Hitchcock<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/110943.Steven_Jacobs?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Steven Jacobs<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.95 avg rating &mdash; 22 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2007\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer9715038580132800960\">In the films of Alfred Hitchcock, architecture plays an important role. Having worked as a set designer in the early 1920s, Hitchcock remained intensely concerned with the art direction of his films. In addition, the ’master of suspense’ made some re<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9715038580132800960\" style=\"display:none\">In the films of Alfred Hitchcock, architecture plays an important role. Having worked as a set designer in the early 1920s, Hitchcock remained intensely concerned with the art direction of his films. In addition, the ’master of suspense’ made some remarkable single-set films, such as Rope and Rear Window, that explicitly deal with the way the confines of the set relate to those of the architecture on screen. Spaces of confinement also turn up in the ’Gothic plot’ of films in which the house is presented as an uncanny labyrinth and a trap. Furthermore, it became a Hitchcock hallmark to use famous monuments as the location for a climactic scene. Last but not least, Hitchcock used architectural motifs such as stairs and windows, which are closely connected to Hitchcockian narrative structures (suspense) or typical Hitchcock themes (voyeurism). Apart from dealing with these issues extensively, Steven Jacobs discusses at length a series of domestic buildings with the help of a number of reconstructed floor plans especially made for this publication. \n\nSteven Jacobs is an art historian who has published widely on the photographic and cinematic representations of architecture, cities, and landscapes. He currently lectures on film history at Sint Lukas College of Art Brussels and the Academy of Fine Arts in Ghent, and on urban studies at Erasmus University Rotterdam.<\/span>\n  <a Hoe kon de vroegere, relatief rustige kolonie van België, sinds 1960 onafhankelijk, zo veranderen? David Van Reybrouck beschrijft voor het eerst de verbijsterende geschiedenis van Congo, van ruim voor de komst van de ontdekkingsreiziger Stanley tot en met de invloed van China in de laatste tien jaar en de recente economische crisis. Van 1885 tot 1908 werd het land bestierd door koning Leopold ii, die een fortuin verdiende met de exploitatie van rubber.\n\nDe kolonisatie door België in de periode van 1908 tot 1960 zorgde voor industrialisatie en infrastructuur, maar werd ook gekenmerkt door paternalisme, zo niet betutteling. De onverhoedse overgang van kolonie naar onafhankelijke staat rond 1960 is een adembenemend verhaal vol idealisme en gekonkel. Het nieuwe land stortte zich in een turbulent avontuur dat steeds wilder en chaotischer werd en na tweeëndertig jaar dictatuur onder Mobutu leidde tot een van de dodelijkste conflicten sinds de Tweede Wereldoorlog. Van Reybrouck baseert zich niet alleen op zeldzaam archiefmateriaal en baanbrekend onderzoek, maar vooral ook op honderden gesprekken die hij met Congolezen voerde.\n\nZijn ooggetuigen gaan van eeuwlingen tot kindsoldaten, van rebellenleiders tot smokkelaars, van ministers tot maniokverkoopsters. Robbert groeit op, probeert zijn moeder te plezieren, maar ze wijst hem telkens af. Hij probeert mooie momenten met zijn vader te sprokkelen, maar het steekspel met zijn moeder domineert zijn leven.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/130272.Charles_Palliser">About Charles Palliser</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/130272.Charles_Palliser"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1264721185p3/130272.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/130272.Charles_Palliser">Charles Palliser
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
165
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2802321268013953059">Charles Palliser (born 1947) is an American-born, British-based novelist. He is the elder brother of the late author and freelance journalist <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1173083.Marcus_Palliser' title='Marcus Palliser' rel='nofollow noopener'>Marcus Palliser</a>.<br/><br/>Born in New England he is an American citizen but has lived in the United Kingdom since the age of three. He went up to Oxford in 1967 to read English Language and Literature and took a First in June 1970. He was awarded the B. Litt. in 1975 </span>
  <span id="freeText2802321268013953059" style="display:none">Charles Palliser (born 1947) is an American-born, British-based novelist. He is the elder brother of the late author and freelance journalist <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1173083.Marcus_Palliser" title="Marcus Palliser" rel="nofollow noopener">Marcus Palliser</a>.<br /><br />Born in New England he is an American citizen but has lived in the United Kingdom since the age of three. He went up to Oxford in 1967 to read English Language and Literature and took a First in June 1970. He was awarded the B. Litt. in 1975 for a dissertation on Modernist fiction.<br /><br />From 1974 until 1990 Palliser was a Lecturer in the Department of English at the University of Strathclyde in Glasgow. He was the first Deputy Editor of The Literary Review when it was founded in 1979. He taught creative writing during the Spring semester of 1986 at Rutgers University in New Jersey. In 1990 he gave up his university post to become a full-time writer when his first novel, <i>The Quincunx</i>, became an international best-seller. He has published four novels which have been translated into a dozen languages.<br /><br />Palliser has also written for the theatre, radio, and television. His stage play, <i>Week Nothing</i>, toured Scotland in 1980. His 90 minute radio play, <i>The Journal of Simon Owen</i>, was commissioned by the BBC and twice broadcast on Radio 4 in June, 1982. His short TV film, <i>Obsessions: Writing</i>, was broadcast by the BBC and published by BBC Publications in 1991. Most recently, his short radio play, <i>Artist with Designs</i>, was broadcast on BBC Radio 3 on 21 February 2004.<br /><br />He teaches occasionally for the Arvon Foundation, the Skyros Institute, London University, the London Metropolitan University, and Middlesex University. He was Writer in Residence at Poitiers University in 1997.<br />In 1991 <i>The Quincunx</i> was awarded the Sue Kaufman Prize for First Fiction by the American Academy and Institute of Arts and Letters which is given for the best first novel published in North America. <i>The Unburied</i> was nominated for the 2001 International IMPAC Dublin Literary Award.<br />Since 1990 he has written the Introduction to a Penguin Classics edition of the Sherlock Holmes stories, the Foreword to a new French translation of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4012.Wilkie_Collins_" title="Wilkie Collins’" rel="nofollow noopener">Wilkie Collins’</a> <i>The Moonstone</i> published by Editions Phebus, and other articles on 19th century and contemporary fiction. He is a past member of the long-running North London Writers circle.<br /><br />From <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Palliser" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2802321268013953059" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
